Nonprofit Hospitals, Once For the Poor, Strike It Rich --- With Tax Breaks, They Outperform For-Profit Rivals
Nonprofit Hospitals, Once For the Poor, Strike It Rich --- With Tax Breaks, They Outperform For-Profit
Rivals
By John Carreyrou and Barbara Martinez
2,928 words
4 April 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Nonprofit hospitals, originally set up to serve the poor, have transformed themselves into profit machines.
And as the money rolls in, the large tax breaks they receive are drawing fire.
Riding gains from investment portfolios and enjoying the pricing power that came from a decade of mergers,
many nonprofit hospitals have seen earnings soar in recent years. The combined net income of the 50 largest
nonprofit hospitals jumped nearly eight-fold to $4.27 billion between 2001 and 2006, according to a Wall
Street Journal analysis of data from the American Hospital Directory. AHD, an information-service company,
compiles data that hospitals report to the federal government.
The Cleveland Clinic swung from a loss to net income of $229 million during that period. No fewer than 25
nonprofit hospitals or hospital systems now earn more than $250 million a year. One nonprofit hospital
system, Ascension Health, has a treasure chest of $7.4 billion -- more than many large, publicly traded
companies.
Nonprofits, which account for a majority of U.S. hospitals, are faring even better than their for-profit
counterparts: 77% of the 2,033 U.S. nonprofit hospitals are in the black, while just 61% of for-profit hospitals
are profitable, according to the AHD data.
At some nonprofits, the good times are reflected in new facilities and rich executive pay. Flush with cash,
Northwestern Memorial Hospital in Chicago has rebuilt its entire campus since 1999 at a cost of more than $1
billion. In October, it opened a new women's hospital that features marble in the lobby, birthing rooms with
flat-screen televisions, 1,000 works of art and a roof topped with 10,000 square feet of gardens. In 2006,
Northwestern Memorial's former chief executive officer, Gary Mecklenburg, received a $16.4 million payout.
But Northwestern Memorial has been frugal in its spending on charity care, the free treatment for poor
patients that nonprofit hospitals are expected to provide in return for the federal and state tax breaks they
receive. In 2006, Northwestern Memorial spent $20.8 million on charity care -- less than 2% of its revenues
and a fraction of what it received in tax breaks. By comparison, the hospitals run by Cook County, where
Northwestern Memorial is located, spent 14% of revenues on charity care.
Northwestern Memorial says that in addition to charity care, it provides other benefits to its community, such
as pioneering research in obstetrics and other areas that improve standards of care nationally.
To be sure, some nonprofit hospitals, particularly ones in inner cities that handle large numbers of uninsured
patients, remain under financial strain and are struggling to keep their doors open.
But the growing gap between many nonprofit hospitals' wealth and what they give back to their communities
is raising questions about the billions of dollars in tax exemptions they receive.
"Some nonprofit hospitals seem to forget that their operations are subsidized with generous tax breaks. They
allow their priorities to get out of whack," says Sen. Charles Grassley. The senior Republican on the Senate
Finance Committee threatened last year to introduce legislation forcing nonprofit hospitals to provide a
minimum amount of charity care.
Nonprofit hospitals account for about 60% of the more than 3,400 hospitals in the U.S. The rest are either
for-profit or government-owned.
In a report issued in December 2006, the Congressional Budget Office estimated nonprofit hospitals receive
$12.6 billion in annual tax exemptions, on top of the $32 billion in federal, state and local subsidies the
hospital industry as a whole receives each year.

--- Page 203 ---
Page 203 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In return for not paying taxes, nonprofit hospitals are supposed to provide a "community benefit," a loosely
defined requirement whose most important component is charity care. But many hospitals include other
expenses in their community-benefit accounting to the Internal Revenue Service, including unpaid patient
bills. Often, hospitals also include the difference between the list prices of treatment they provide and what
they are paid by Medicaid and Medicare, the government programs for the poor, disabled and elderly.
Excluding those other expenses, many hospitals spend less on charity care than they get in tax breaks,
studies by various counties and states show.
One nonprofit hospital system, St. Louis-based BJC HealthCare, counts the salaries of its employees as a
community benefit. BJC, which runs 14 hospitals in Missouri and Illinois, says on its Web site that it provided
more than $1.8 billion in benefits to various communities in 2004. Its payroll, including its CEO's $1.8 million
compensation, accounted for $937 million of that figure, while charity care represented $35 million, according
to BJC.
"The impact that any organization that's job-producing and buying goods has on a community is of benefit to
that community," says BJC HealthCare spokeswoman June Fowler. However, she says BJC won't count its
payroll as a community benefit in the future because of new standards adopted by the IRS.
The new standards, due to take full effect in 2009, will require nonprofit hospitals to break out specifics of
their community-benefit contributions. But they won't require the hospitals to provide any minimum amount of
charity care.
The size of nonprofit hospitals' tax exemptions is coming under scrutiny in part because their incomes have
risen so sharply in recent years, and because they represent such a big chunk of America's health-care
spending. Thirty-one cents of every dollar spent on medical care is spent on hospitals.
One reason for hospitals' soaring profits is a gradual increase in Medicare reimbursements after federal
budget cutbacks during the 1990s. By merging and gaining scale, many hospitals also gained leverage in
price negotiations with health insurers.
However, much of the industry's profit growth comes from strategies it honed to increase profits. Among
them: demanding upfront payments from patients; hiking list prices for procedures and services to several
times their actual cost; selling patients' debts to collection companies; focusing on expensive procedures; and
issuing tax-exempt bonds and investing the proceeds in higher-yielding securities.
Untaxed investment gains have greatly increased some hospitals' cash piles. Ascension Health, a Catholic
nonprofit system that runs 65 hospitals, mostly in the Midwest and Northeast, reported net income of $1.2
billion in its fiscal year ended June 30, 2007, and cash and investments of $7.4 billion. That's more cash than
Walt Disney Co. has.
Ascension says it needs to maintain a sufficient amount of cash to pay for charity care, to keep the interest
rates it pays on its debt low, to provide retirement benefits to its 106,000 employees, and to make capital and
technology investments at its hospitals.
At the University of Pittsburgh Medical Center, which runs 20 facilities, cash and investments totaled $3.35
billion at the end of last year. UPMC says the money goes toward producing "world-class health care,
education and research," citing the $1 billion it spent over five years to create electronic medical records for
patients and an additional $500 million to build a children's hospital and a network of cancer centers.
But some of UPMC's expenses are only tenuously related to medicine. In its 2006 fiscal year, UPMC also
spent $10 million on advertising, including $1 million on ads in the New York Times. Wendy Zellner, a
spokeswoman for the hospital, says the ads enable UPMC "to better compete with other leading hospitals."
UPMC paid its CEO, Jeffrey Romoff, $3.3 million in fiscal 2006. Mr. Romoff also received $36,995 from the
hospital to cover a car allowance, spousal travel and legal and financial counseling. Ms. Zellner says what
UPMC pays Mr. Romoff is in line with "nonprofit and for-profit organizations of comparable scope and
complexity."
Some nonprofit hospital executives enjoy other perks. Royal Oak, Mich.-based Beaumont Hospitals says it
paid $10,795 for the country-club membership of the president of its foundation last year. A spokeswoman for
Beaumont says it pays for the membership to provide the executive "a venue with access to potential
donors."
The Cleveland Clinic continued to pay its former CEO, Floyd Loop, more than $1 million a year for two years
after he retired in April 2005. The Cleveland Clinic says part of that was deferred compensation and vacation
pay and the rest was for consulting services.

--- Page 204 ---
Page 204 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The University of California San Francisco Medical Center provided its CEO and chief operating officer
low-interest mortgage loans of more than $1 million each, according to the University of California's executive
compensation reports. A UCSF spokeswoman says such loans help recruit and retain executives, given the
area's high cost of housing.
Catholic Healthcare West, a hospital system based in San Francisco, forgave a $782,541 housing loan it
made to its CEO, Lloyd Dean. Counting the forgiven loan, Mr. Dean's total accrued compensation in 2005
was $5.8 million. Catholic Healthcare West says his compensation reflects his skill in turning the hospital
system around financially.
One nonprofit hospital executive who has benefited from the industry's good fortunes is Mr. Mecklenburg, the
former CEO of Chicago's Northwestern Memorial. The hospital says it paid him $5.45 million in salary, bonus
and deferred compensation in its fiscal year ended Aug. 31, 2006, and an additional $10.95 million when he
retired the next day. The hospital also awarded five other executives a combined $13.3 million in total
compensation in fiscal 2006, according to its filings to the IRS.
Mr. Mecklenburg, now a partner at Chicago private-equity firm Waud Capital Partners LLC, declined to
comment, referring questions to the hospital and to the former chairman of its compensation committee,
James Denny.
Northwestern Memorial says a big part of Mr. Mecklenburg's $16.4 million payout represents retirement
benefits and deferred compensation accrued over his 21-year tenure. Mr. Denny, who chaired the hospital's
compensation committee from 1995 to January 2008, says Mr. Mecklenburg delivered stellar results, nearly
quintupling the hospital's patient revenues. "Our view of it is: This is the best deal we've ever made," he says.
Critics argue that Mr. Mecklenburg's compensation is excessive for a charity organization that gets tens of
millions of dollars a year in tax breaks. Northwestern Memorial sits on property on the Gold Coast, Chicago's
most affluent neighborhood, abutting Lake Michigan. The Center for Tax and Budget Accountability, a
Chicago nonprofit organization, estimates the value of the hospital's annual property-tax exemption at $37.5
million. Northwestern Memorial is also exempt from $12.5 million in sales tax for a total of $50 million in
annual tax exemptions, not counting the taxes it doesn't pay on its investment gains, the center estimates.
"The hospital's tax benefit is more than two times greater than the charity care provided," says Heather
O'Donnell, the center's health-care policy director.
Northwestern Memorial says it hasn't calculated the value of its tax exemptions. Robert Christie, the hospital's
vice president for government relations, notes that the Center for Tax and Budget Accountability receives
funding from the Service Employees International Union, which represents numerous hospital employees and
frequently clashes with hospitals in labor disputes. Ms. O'Donnell says her organization receives funding from
many foundations besides SEIU.
Peter McCanna, Northwestern Memorial's chief financial officer, says the hospital's contribution to its
community should be judged more broadly. "We fundamentally disagree with narrowing [the definition of] our
community-benefit contribution to charity care," he says. He says Northwestern Memorial's research and
education expenses should also be counted. The hospital is the primary teaching hospital for Northwestern
University's Feinberg School of Medicine.
Taking into account educational and other expenses, such as bad debt and unreimbursed Medicaid costs,
Northwestern Memorial values its total community-benefit contribution at $230 million for fiscal 2006.
Around Chicago, Northwestern Memorial is known as a hospital that attracts the well-heeled. It's a short walk
from the Magnificent Mile, the famous thoroughfare lined with expensive shops and restaurants. At
Northwestern Memorial's new Prentice Women's Hospital, expectant mothers can watch TV or browse the
Internet on 42-inch flat-screen televisions, order room service 24 hours a day and page nurses and doctors
via a wireless system. Some birthing rooms have views of Lake Michigan. Only 6% of Northwestern
Memorial's patient revenues come from Medicaid.
By comparison, Sacred Heart Hospital, a small for-profit hospital in a poor neighborhood on the west side of
the city, gets 62% of its revenues from Medicaid and pays several million dollars a year in taxes, according to
its president, Edward Novak. Parts of Sacred Heart date back to 1928, when the hospital was founded.
Another wing was built in 1950. Mr. Novak says he would like to replace the aging hospital with a new facility,
but is struggling to figure out how to pay for it. He says his compensation is less than $220,000 a year.
At John H. Stroger Jr. Hospital -- formerly known as Cook County Hospital -- 56% of patients don't have any
insurance when they are admitted, says John Cookinham, the hospital's chief financial officer. At
Northwestern Memorial, the percentage of uninsured patients is less than 5%. Stroger's chief operating officer
earned $204,485 in 2007, according to Cook County budget records.

--- Page 205 ---
Page 205 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In recent years, some nonprofit hospitals have decided to stop using the courts to collect from patients who
owe them money. But Northwestern Memorial pursues patients such as Iris Ayala who haven't paid their bills.
While running an errand for her employer, the 50-year-old Ms. Ayala fainted and collapsed in the street one
day in 2006. A friend rushed her to Northwestern Memorial's emergency room.
Ms. Ayala says her insurer paid for the bulk of her 24-hour hospital stay, but she was responsible for a
$1,035.39 co-pay. Working only part-time because of health issues and with a daughter in college, she says
she couldn't afford her portion of the bill.
After representatives for Northwestern Memorial repeatedly called her to ask for payment, Ms. Ayala says
she promised she would settle the bill once she got her annual tax refund. But Northwestern Memorial sued
her in Cook County Circuit Court in July 2007. To make the lawsuit go away, Ms. Ayala says she borrowed
the money and paid the hospital. "They didn't want to hear my sob story," she says.
Northwestern Memorial declined to discuss Ms. Ayala's case, citing patient privacy laws. Mr. McCanna says
the hospital sued only 82 patients in 2006 and 2007, a number he says is small compared with the more than
one million accounts it billed over that period. He says the hospital tries to determine whether patients who
are behind on bills qualify for assistance, but some can't be reached or refuse to volunteer information about
their finances. "Absent of information, a lawsuit is sometimes the only recourse," he says. Mr. McCanna adds
that, in some cases, the hospital has waived patients' bills after later learning that they did qualify for aid.
Northwestern Memorial says its strong balance sheet allows it to provide outstanding care and conduct
innovative research. As of Aug. 31, 2007, its cash and investments totaled $1.82 billion, making it one of
richest individual nonprofit hospitals in the country. With such a treasure chest, it could operate for a year and
two months without any revenue -- a gauge of financial strength Mr. McCanna highlights in presentations to
bond investors and analysts.
"Nonprofit is a misnomer -- it's nontaxable," says Sacred Heart Hospital's Mr. Novak. "When you're making
hundreds of millions of dollars a year, how can you call yourself a not-for-profit?"
---
From Charity and Tax Breaks to Healthy Profits
Historically, most hospitals in America have been recognized as "charitable organizations" exempt from taxes
under Section 501(c)(3) of the U.S. Tax Code. In return for their tax exemptions, the Internal Revenue
Service used to require that nonprofit hospitals provide a substantial amount of care for the poor, also known
as charity care.
But after Medicare and Medicaid were created in 1965, the hospital industry contended that there would no
longer be enough demand for charity care to satisfy the IRS's tax-exemption standards. Most Americans, it
argued, would be covered either by the new government programs for the poor and elderly or by private
health insurance. The industry pushed for a more flexible exemption standard that became known as the
"community benefit" standard. The IRS adopted it in 1969.
Some charity care has continued to be expected of nonprofit hospitals as part of their contributions to their
communities, but no minimum amount has been required.
Today, about 60% of the 3,400 hospitals in the U.S. are nonprofit. About 23% of hospitals are for-profit, and
another 17% are run by counties, states or the federal government.
Like other nonprofit organizations, nonprofit hospitals don't have shareholders or owners to whom any profits
can be distributed. Rather, any money they earn from their operations or from financial investments must be
channeled back into the organization in some way. Many nonprofit hospitals have used their growing
surpluses to pay for expensive new facilities and equipment, to reward their executives with large pay
packages and to increase their treasure chests.

World News: Zimbabwe Election Results Encourage Investors --- Die-Hard Group Finds Vindication In

Official results released Wednesday from Zimbabwe's weekend election showed President Robert Mugabe's
ruling party losing its control of Parliament, cheering opposition candidates and critics of the autocratic leader.
Another group heartened by the news: international investors.
Even as Zimbabwe suffers the world's highest inflation rate, Mr. Mugabe's erratic economic policies and,
recently, a threat by the government to seize control of foreign firms, a handful of die-hard investors have
been betting the country can turn itself around. Mr. Mugabe's electoral misfortunes may make that more
likely.
Official results in the presidential vote haven't been released yet by Zimbabwe's electoral commission,
although the opposition claimed victory, based on independent polling results.
Two months ago, Washington-based private-equity firm Emerging Capital Partners bought an 18% stake in
Central African Gold PLC, a Ghana-based company that owns a majority share of Falcon Gold Zimbabwe
Ltd., a Zimbabwe gold miner.
"Nobody thought it was going to be easy to bring change to Zimbabwe, and it is proving difficult," said Navaid
Burney, the firm's managing director. But amid the election news, "I feel good about our investments,
anticipating what's to come," he says.
Late Wednesday, Zimbabwe's electoral commission said the opposition Movement for Democratic Change
had won 105 of Parliament's 210 seats, ending control of the body by Mr. Mugabe's party, which so far has
captured 94. An independent candidate won one seat.
Separately, the opposition said it had won the presidency, too. It based its claim on a report from an
independent polling group that currently shows opposition leader Morgan Tsvangirai with 50.3% of the vote,
compared with 42.9% for Mr. Mugabe and 6.8% for the president's former finance minister, Simba Makoni.
The independent pollsters compiled results from polling stations to get their tally, and said that all but one
precinct has reported. The electoral law requires a candidate to have a "majority" of votes to win outright,
otherwise a run-off must be held between the top two candidates in 21 days.
The opposition Wednesday said it would accept a run-off. Mr. Mugabe's party, the Zimbabwe African National
Unity-Patriotic Front, has dismissed the victory claims, saying it is waiting for the official results. But in what
appears at least as a grudging acknowledgement of the electoral blow so far, a government-aligned
newspaper Wednesday forecast a run-off between the two, saying that voting patterns so far indicated neither
would capture enough votes to avoid one.
The delay in announcing the results has brought cries of foul play from the opposition and strong statements
from the international community. The acknowledgment of a likely run-off from the state press was interpreted
cynically by many in the opposition as another attempt by Mr. Mugabe to buy time and hold onto power.
Mr. Mugabe has ruled the country for 28 years, since independence, and at 84 years old wasn't expected to
accept defeat, fairly or otherwise. But his greatly weakened political fortunes this week have suddenly
heartened many Zimbabweans, eager to cast off his often-brutal rule and anxious to revitalize a wilting
economy.
Mr. Mugabe's slipping grip has also vindicated a small group of outside investors in the country. Investment
house Lonrho PLC and Botswana-based investment banking company Imara Holdings Ltd. each set up
Zimbabwe-focused funds last year at the request of their clients. Russian investment bank Renaissance

--- Page 209 ---

Capital, which has recently refocused to invest in Africa, announced a stock index covering 11 markets in
sub-Saharan Africa, including Zimbabwe.
Pouring money into an economy with over 100,000% inflation, a crumbling infrastructure, a fleeing work
force and a government anxious to nationalize foreign companies might not seem like a great idea. But
quietly in the past year, a handful of international investors have done just that.
They have snapped up companies at fire-sale prices, many of which were selling, according to one fund
manager's estimate, at 15%-20% of their true value. Of course, even valuing how much they paid for their
investments was tricky, as the local currency collapsed. One U.S. dollar will now get you 54,640,143
Zimbabwean dollars as of Wednesday, a big difference from the government's official, fixed rate of US$1 to
Z$30,000.
Still, some of the country's major draws are hard to ignore amid a commodities boom across Africa. The
country boasts vast mining reserves and a dilapidated, but promising, tourism industry.
Imara, for example, has made some of its biggest Zimbabwean investments in mining subsidiary Rio Tinto
Zimbabwe Ltd. and in Dawn Properties Ltd., a tourism property company based in Zimbabwe.
Of course, Mr. Mugabe is still in power, and it could take some time for the economy to rebound even if he
stepped down immediately. But investors argue that Zimbabwe, with its platinum and gold reserves, skilled
work force anxious to start anew, and prosperous neighbors, will quickly rise again. And the currently
discounted prices for its companies could do the same.
A new regime would also likely have to renegotiate, or at least clarify, the country's fresh nationalization law,
signed by Mr. Mugabe last month to allow locals to take a majority stake in foreign companies.
The government has been unclear about how the new law would be implemented, but it threatened the
extensive international mining operations still in the country, as well as major companies such as British
American Tobacco PLC and Coca-Cola Co. and

World News: Mugabe's Grip Slips in Zimbabwe --- Opponent Has Lead In Presidential Vote, Tallies Indicate
World News: Mugabe's Grip Slips in Zimbabwe --- Opponent Has Lead In Presidential Vote, Tallies
Indicate
By Sarah Childress
957 words
2 April 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A7
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Independent election tallies in Zimbabwe continued to show the opposition party ahead, dealing a major blow
to the prospect that President Robert Mugabe will hold on to power.
Late Tuesday, opposition leaders denied reports from Harare, the capital, that Mr. Mugabe or his lieutenants
were in talks with them about the president stepping down.
Still, a strong showing in Saturday's polls has emboldened the opposition like in no other recent election. Past
elections have been marred by widespread allegations of voter manipulation.
That Mr. Mugabe, who has ruled Zimbabwe for 28 years, has allowed the appearance of weakness in the
election marks a surprising turn of events. The president has long been seen as unwilling to relinquish power.
In past voting, early leads touted by the opposition quickly turned in Mr. Mugabe's favor.
Preliminary results collected by independent pollsters put the opposition Movement for Democratic Change's
leader, Morgan Tsvangirai, ahead with 50% of the vote. That included tallies from 91% of precincts.
Mr. Mugabe polls at 43%, and Simba Makoni, the president's former finance minister, who entered the race
last month, trails with 7%, according to those tallies.
The MDC said its own surveys were similar but that Mr. Tsvangirai had reached the 51% threshold the
constitution requires to avoid a runoff of the top two finishers.
Official parliamentary votes have been trickling out, showing government and opposition candidates neck and
neck for control of the assembly. But no official numbers have been released in the presidential vote.
In the run-up to the vote, international watchdog groups cited several incidents of ruling-party supporters
beating or harassing opposition supporters and bribing rural voters with state-subsidized food. Such behavior
has become typical of elections in Zimbabwe, according to international monitors.
But as results came in during this election, even members of the MDC, accustomed to electoral abuse,
showed unusual confidence. Many questioned whether Zimbabwe's security services -- in the past loyaly
aligned with Mr. Mugabe -- would play along with election-tampering this time around.
Mr. Mugabe "controls the army, he controls the police and the intelligence," said Tendai Biti, the
secretary-general of the MDC. "But the majority of soldiers, the majority of people serving in the police force
are sick and tired and want change."
For the past nine years, Zimbabwe's economy has contracted under Mr. Mugabe and his erratic policies,
despite vast gold and platinum reserves. But recently, economic hardship has turned into catastrophe.
Zimbabwe's cities black out so regularly that people often use firewood to prepare meals. Running water is
scarce, and supermarkets are often empty. Earlier this year, inflation reached 100,000%.
Zimbabweans, among the most educated and literate people in the region, have watched as their neighbors
have enjoyed the fruits of a global commodities boom. South Africa, with its developed infrastructure, became
sub-Saharan Africa's commercial hub. Angola, though recovering from a civil war, is awash in oil revenue,
and Zambia's copper mines are humming. Tourists who once visited Zimbabwe to see the thundering Victoria
Falls, one of Africa's most impressive sights, now view them instead from the Zambian side of the border.
Perhaps the starkest contrast to Zimbabwe is its next-door neighbor: peaceful, diamond-rich Botswana, which
has the highest gross domestic product, per capita, in southern Africa. On Tuesday, Botswana saw a smooth

--- Page 212 ---
Page 212 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
transition of power from the country's retiring president, who is credited with helping nurture Botswana's
prosperous, middle-class economy.
Meanwhile, Zimbabwe's failing economy has threatened the prosperity of these same neighbors. That would
make a changing of the guard -- so long as it is peaceful -- a welcome development in the region.
By last year, nearly a quarter of Zimbabwe's population had fled the country, some braving crocodiles as they
crossed the Limpopo River in search of work in neighboring South Africa. There, Zimbabwean professionals
trained as teachers or doctors regularly work in menial jobs, sending food and other goods back home.
Zimbabwe's political future will also have a major impact on the operations of international companies still in
the country, several of them major mining companies, such as Rio Tinto PLC and Anglo American PLC. and
This month Mr. Mugabe signed a bill that would require foreign companies to hand over a majority stake to
locals. The effort was seen as a populist bid as elections neared. It was unclear what the specifics of the law
will be or how it will be implemented.
Analysts compared the bill with the government's 2000 decision to seize commercial farms, predicting it
would further contribute to the deterioration of the economy if companies felt threatened enough to suspend
operations there.
The weekend election in Zimbabwe was largely peaceful. But events in nearby Kenya -- where a relatively
peaceful election late last year spawned ethnic violence amid widespread allegations of vote rigging -- have
hung over the Zimbabwe election.
Observers reported the names of several thousand nonexistent voters on the rolls on election day. The MDC
reported some of its supporters had been turned away at polling stations.
The European Union Tuesday urged Mr. Mugabe to step down to avoid political turmoil. "If Mr. Mugabe
continues, there will be a coup d'etat," Slovenian Foreign Minister Dimitri Rupel, whose country holds the EU
presidency, told reporters. Riot police patrolled the streets of the capital.
The opposition has accused the electoral commission of delaying the results to give the government time to
orchestrate a win. The commissioner said there were technical complications because it had to certify
presidential-, local- and parliamentary-election results at the same time.

Small-capitalization stocks rose sharply Tuesday, as momentum from a financial-led surge among large caps
carried over to their smaller, more volatile brethren.
"That end-of-the-world, doom-and-gloom pessimism that was priced into the market may have been
overstated," said Stephen Wood, senior portfolio strategist for Russell Investments.
The Russell 2000 index rose 22.67 points, or 3.3%, to 710.64, leaving it up 10% from its 2008 closing low of
643.97 on March 10. The Standard & Poor's SmallCap 600 gained 11.92 points, or 3.3%, to 376.51.
Both indexes posted their highest close since Feb. 27.
The broader market welcomed capital-raising plans from large-cap UBS and Lehman Brothers as a sign that
beaten-down financials are taking steps to strengthen their balance sheets.
With turmoil in the housing market, a credit crunch and continued talk of a recession, investors have turned to
larger names for a safe haven, Mr. Wood said. But that strategy may be changing.
"We're seeing risk capital move away from safe-haven strategies," he said.
Many small-cap financial names followed Lehman and UBS higher, including LaBranche & Co. (New York
Stock Exchange), which rose 56 cents, or 13%, to 4.91; Piper Jaffray (NYSE), up 4.03, or 12%, to 37.99; and
World Acceptance added 3.26, or 10%, to 35.11.
Thornburg Mortgage (NYSE) rose 24 cents, or 20%, to 1.45, after the residential-finance company said it
succeeded in raising new capital to avert bankruptcy. Thornburg said it raised $1.35 billion through selling
bonds, warrants to purchase its common shares and interests in certain mortgage assets.
SWS Group (NYSE), which completed its acquisition of M.L. Stern & Co. from Pacific Life Insurance, finished
up 1.56, or 13%, at 12.76.
Bucking the trend for financials was KKR Financial Holdings, which fell 1.10, or 8.7%, to 11.56 on the NYSE.
The San Francisco specialty-finance company said it will sell 20 million new shares and it transferred
mortgage-backed securities to holders of its commercial paper. In return, those noteholders will release KKR
Financial from its obligations to repay them.
Many consumer-discretionary stocks also rose as commodity prices declined, with oil and gold losing ground
as the dollar strengthened against the euro. Light, sweet crude for May delivery settled 0.6% lower at $100.98
a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange, while Comex gold for April delivery fell 3.6%.
Consumer-discretionary stocks that moved higher included Finish Line, which gained 1.06, or 22%, to 5.82;
Tuesday Morning, up 60 cents, or 12%, to 5.78; and Standard Motor Products (NYSE), which finished up 59
cents, or 9.6%, at 6.71.
Buffalo Wild Wings added 3, or 12%, to 27.50, after Morgan Keegan raised its rating on the Minneapolis
restaurant operator to outperform from market perform.
Talbots (NYSE) gained 1.23, or 11%, to 12.01. The Hingham, Mass., women's clothing retailer unveiled a
turnaround plan that includes inventory trimming, improved merchandise and a possible move into the outlet
segment.

said it received about $58 million from the sale of its SP Newsprint joint venture to White Birch Paper. The
company said it will use funds from the sale to reduce its debt by about $38 million.
Ceradyne rose 2.87, or 9%, to 34.83, after the Costa Mesa, Calif., maker of ceramic-body armor said it

Gold Slides Back Below $900
Commodities Report
Gold Slides Back Below $900
By Allen Sykora
632 words
2 April 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C7
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold's sharp decline is still being viewed as a correction within a bull market, even though the metal has now
posted two substantial single-day dollar declines in the past two weeks.
Prices on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange fell Tuesday as the dollar strengthened
for the day and the market broke through the psychologically important $900, which triggered further selling.
Nearby April gold slid $33.30 an ounce to $882.90, while most-active June fell $33.70 to $887.80.
June gold traded as low as $876.30 after hitting a life-of-contract high of $1,038.60 on March 17. Tuesday's
break came on the heels of a March 19 selloff, when spot-month gold tumbled $58.50, the largest one-day
dollar decline since January 1980.
"You've got to bear in mind the spectacular nature of the rally [prior to the pullback the past two weeks]," said
Jim Steel, metals analyst with HSBC. "It's down only a little over 15% from the high. That's still a correction.
"The bull market is still intact, although I'm not saying we're going to go back to the highs. As far as I can tell,
the bull rally in commodities in general has not ended."
Further U.S. rate cuts are likely to mean further dollar weakness, which tends to support gold, said Bart
Melek, commodities strategist with BMO Capital Markets. Additionally, inflation remains a concern,
particularly due to high fuel and food costs, and gold is often bought as a hedge against inflation.
"I see this as a profit-taking correction," Mr. Melek said. "My suspicion is the Federal Reserve is going to have
to lower rates further. There continues to be a credit crunch in the banking system, and at the same time the
U.S. economy is not doing well."
Michael Gross, broker and futures analyst with OptionSellers.com, said the correction and consolidation in
gold could continue for four to six weeks.
The dollar might recover some more in the short term, he said. If so, there could be more long liquidation of
speculative fund positions in gold and silver. Mr. Gross said speculators are still heavily net long in these
metals. Long positions are expectations prices will rise.
"But I don't think the longer-term bull market is over," Mr. Gross said. "We remain somewhat bearish on the
dollar over the longer term, which should support metals."
Charles Nedoss, senior account manager and metals analyst with Peak Trading Group, figures gold may
have to rally somewhere in the neighborhood of $50 from current levels to really encourage the bulls to return
to the market. But, like others, he described Tuesday's selloff as a profit-taking correction.
"If you look at the weekly charts and the monthly charts, the uptrend is still intact," Mr. Nedoss said. It was
"positive" that the funds reduced their net length considerably in the last reporting period for Commodity
Futures Trading Commission data, Mr. Nedoss said. "And I'm sure you've had them come out of a lot of it
today."
The CFTC each week reports the position of both speculators and commercial users for commodities. Net
length is the number of total long positions minus total short positions. Analysts often note that a decline in
net length means more potential buyers waiting in the wings for future upswings.
The most recent positioning data from the CFTC showed that the large noncommercials trimmed their net
length in gold futures and options to 190,663 contracts as of March 25, the first time it was below 200,000
since Christmas. It had been as high as 224,528 on Jan. 15.

Puppetry With a (Basil) Twist
LEISURE & ARTS
Puppetry With a (Basil) Twist
By Matthew Gurewitsch
1,627 words
2 April 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
D8
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
New York -- Basil Twist, master puppeteer, stands a whisker shy of six feet tall, with the build of a stick figure,
the hands of a mechanic and a face made to order for the flamboyant pen of Saul Steinberg. Born in San
Francisco 38 years ago, he is a New Yorker now, long settled in a pocket of SoHo that conjures up the
Greenwich Village of the beatniks, long gone. Existentialist black jeans and sweaters underscore the affinity.
All he needs now is the beret and battered paperback of Sartre.
"It's going to be very tight," Mr. Twist warns, speaking in italics, as he often does, inviting an observer into the
cramped bowels of a Leroy Street basement, where he has carved out his studio. Seven years after its
opening night, he is reviving his "Petrushka" for a two-week run, beginning tonight, at the Clark Studio
Theater at Lincoln Center (through April 13). Stand up, and heat blazing off the pipes singes the eyebrows.
Sit low, and icy drafts from the open window send shivers up the spinal column.
But conditions in Mr. Twist's creative sanctum are much, much harder on the operators: three for Petrushka,
that woebegone Russian Pierrot; three for the vainglorious, macho Blackamoor; and three for the haughty
Ballerina they both desire. In a modification of the traditional bunraku technique of Japan, one operator works
a puppet's head and torso, a second the arms, a third the feet. For special effects, everyone pitches in as
available. Mr. Twist lays hands on only in emergencies, as a stand-in. As designer and director, he normally
watches from the "house" -- in this case all of two feet from the front edge of the boards that serve as the
rehearsal stage. Next to his chair, there's just room for three others. This close, it's hard to see the forest for
the trees, but Mr. Twist misses nothing.
Yes, Mr. Twist's "Petrushka" is adapted from the Ballets Russes classic, and more faithfully than you might
expect. Though the plot is reduced to its three central characters, the story is all there. So is the music, sort
of. The identical twins Julia and Irina Elkina perform a two-piano reduction of Stravinsky's scintillating
orchestral score live and complete.
At the Clark, the puppeteers working the feet will have a bench to sit on, and a trough for their legs. "Here,"
Mr. Twist declares, "the rule is 'Kneel and deal.'" The puppeteer in charge of the Blackamoor's feet, it turns
out, is running late tonight, so the rehearsal starts with a scene for Petrushka and the Ballerina. Petrushka
looks as he did at the Ballets Russes, in his checkerboard pants and the peasant blouse with the diamond
hem. But the Ballerina, in her glittering gold tutu, is a wicked caricature of the glamorous Russian defector
Natalia Makarova, who leapt to freedom in London in 1970. Retreating from the pleading Petrushka, she
angles her legs like a praying mantis, as predatory as she is wary. When she flees, he flies into a tantrum,
stamping his feet in existential fury.
With the arrival of the ninth puppeteer, Mr. Twist plunges straight into the melee of the Russian Dance. As
staged here, prestissimo, it's a Darwinian contest for center stage. The Ballerina dives into violent
arabesques, Petrushka flings his limbs like a black belt in karate, the Blackamoor flexes his six-pack abs with
savage abandon. Changing places -- the puppets do a lot of that here -- takes frantic scrambling on the part
of their handlers. By the standards of straphangers at rush hour, there's wiggle room to spare, but given the
wild traffic patterns, it's like pro basketball on a ping-pong table. In rehearsal, it's fun to see who's who: a
willowy supermodel type on the Ballerina's arms; a grinning undergraduate, at risk of losing her spectacles,
on the Ballerina's feet; a fellow as round as a pizza chef on the Blackamoor's serpentine torso; a slinky male
dancer with gimlet eyes on the body of Petrushka. In performance, of course, they will be hidden in plain
sight, "invisible" in black velvet from top to toe.
Exactly 10 years ago, Mr. Twist made a big splash with "Symphonie Fantastique," his staging of the Hector
Berlioz classic in a tank no larger than many a home aquarium. From its premiere engagement at the
diminutive SoHo arts complex known as HERE, the show traveled internationally. Many characterized it as an
underwater, abstract puppet show, which wasn't quite right. Though mostly nonfigurative, it wasn't abstract,
not really. With wands, wisps of cloth, translucent cutouts and tubes of plastic, it traced the composer's

--- Page 219 ---
Page 219 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
obsession with Harriet Smithson, the Irish actress he worshipped across the footlights for years, then met and
married. So much the better if you knew the history -- which is the program of the symphony -- but the arc
was crystal clear whether you did or not.
That was the second of seven original productions Mr. Twist lists on www.basiltwist.com . Before that was
"The Araneidae Show" (1993), described as a "solo puppetry extravaganza of showgirls, lonely streets, cats,
deconstructing theaters, sex, despair, magic, metamorphosis, and the fragile web of illusion sometimes called
'reality.'" After "Symphonie Fantastique" came "Petrushka." Since then, Mr. Twist has designed and staged
three operas: De Falla's "Master Peter's Puppet Show" (after an episode in "Don Quixote," 2002), Respighi's
"Bella Dormente Nel Bosco" (on the theme of the Sleeping Beauty, 2005) and Humperdinck's "Hansel and
Gretel" (with a 12-foot witch, 2006). But the most unusual of his creations was "Dogugaeshi" (2004), named
for an obscure tradition from the Japanese island of Awaji. Mr. Twist first learned about it from a 30-second
film clip shown at a puppet festival in France. What fascinated him was the sight of layer beyond layer of
sliding screens, creating a dazzlement of abstract imagery akin to the view through a rectangular
kaleidoscope.
"The whole form is based on the construction of a palace room in perspective," Mr. Twist says. "The clip just
took my breath away. I watched the whole film loop several times to see it again." Only when he went to do
further research in Japan, however, did Mr. Twist discover that dogugaeshi was mostly forgotten and
technically extinct. "I found some torn screens in a museum vault," he says. "That was all. And a beautiful
magical fox, all white and furry. He was the protector of the tradition. He was waiting there in the vault. He
was waiting for me." That fox became the central character and the single traditional puppet in Mr. Twist's
fantasia on the lost tradition. Some viewers have mistaken him for a dragon.
"In December I took 'Dogu' to Japan and visited the Awaji region," Mr. Twist says. "It's a subtle show. It never
elicited hoots and whistles, even in the U.S. And the Japanese are a very polite people. They watched very
quietly. But afterwards, they expressed this incredible gratitude. They were amazed to see an American put
all this effort into an ancient form that Japan had basically forgotten. They're very sensitive to their culture
being erased by modernism. It hadn't been my intention to revive a lost art form. I was simply interested in it.
But reassembling it and bringing it back to Japan -- for them it was like a Renaissance."
By contrast, "Petrushka" was conceived from the start as an hommage. "I was inspired by the sprawling
puppetry productions done in Hungary and Czechoslovakia before the Wall fell," Mr. Twist says. "They
worked on a big stage, with a proscenium of about 40 feet, with 30 or 40 people backstage. My favorite
shows were in Budapest, where there was a National Puppet Theater. Here, most puppeteers you'll meet do
lots of everything. They have to. In Central Europe, everyone used to specialize. There were schools that fed
right into the national company. Every company had a staff to build puppets. They'd have a scenery shop, a
costume shop, a props shop. They had their own writers. I designed 'Petrushka' and directed it and built the
puppets myself, and it isn't on the scale I saw over there. But I was aiming to make a big show. Even if my
10-foot stage was small by comparison with what I saw in Budapest, it was three times wider than the
proscenium for 'Symphonie Fantastique.' So to me, it felt huge."
Shortly before "Symphonie Fantastique" but well before "Petrushka," Julie Taymor's production of "The Lion
King" opened on Broadway, and pundits were predicting that puppets were going to take over the world. As
Ms. Taymor would be the first to tell you, that hasn't happened. Eleven years on, "The Lion King" remains
one-of-a-kind. As Mr. Twist sees it, it's true that puppets have made inroads. But their place is on the fringe.
"Puppets will be appropriated in a theatrical production," he explains. "People say, 'Hey, let's throw in a
puppet!' Since Anthony Minghella's 'Madama Butterfly,' it seems that every new show at the Metropolitan
Opera has to have one. There will be more soon in 'Satyagraha.' I consider myself an advocate for pure
puppetry. I appreciate a puppet show that is built around the very particular experience of being in a room
and seeing inanimate objects come alive. That's what's unique to puppetry. And it's kind of hard to find."
---
Mr. Gurewitsch writes for the Journal on the arts and creative personalities.

Quarterly Markets Review: Commodities May Slide From Lofty Heights
Quarterly Markets Review: Commodities May Slide From Lofty Heights
By Carolyn Cui
968 words
1 April 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C9
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The bubbly rally in commodity prices could be losing some of its froth.
Most commodities managed to finish the quarter substantially higher, but tighter credit and concerns about
economic growth sent prices into a steep decline in mid-March. By the end of the quarter, the question for
investors was whether commodities were still a haven from financial turmoil elsewhere.
"The idea of bubbles bursting is well known, but determining the moment when the mindset changes seems
near impossible," Tobias Levkovich, chief U.S. equity strategist at Citigroup Inc., wrote in a recent research
note.
There were some historic moments during the quarter: crude-oil prices rose above $110 a barrel, topping the
inflation-adjusted high set in 1980; gold prices breached $1,000 for the first time; agricultural products and
other precious metals all hit highs week after week.
By the end of the quarter, wheat futures had risen 5% on the Chicago Board of Trade to $9.29 a bushel, while
platinum traded on the New York Mercantile Exchange had surged 33% to $2,024.40 a troy ounce. The
Nymex's front-month crude-oil contract had risen 5.8% to $101.58 a barrel and the nearby gold contract on
the Comex division of the exchange had climbed 9.7% to $916.20 a troy ounce. Still, most prices ended the
period off their highs for the quarter.
The commodity run-up of the past six years gained speed late last summer, as uncertainty in the credit
markets, growing inflationary pressures and the sagging dollar combined to spark a buying binge of hard
assets. A weaker dollar also contributed to the boom, as most commodities are priced in dollars and
producers could demand higher prices.
Driven by a continuous inflow of cash, the momentum carried into this year, leaving many analysts and
traders wondering whether some commodities were priced too high to be justified by fundamental supply and
demand.
Despite talk of a bubble, mutual funds and exchange-traded funds that invest in commodity futures and in
shares of raw-material companies added $3 billion in net cash since the beginning of this year, one of the few
growing categories of funds, according to EPFR Global, a fund-data provider.
The credit crisis fueled fears that a deep U.S. recession might snuff out the global growth that has driven the
commodities rally. The demise of Bear Stearns Cos. poured more cold water onto commodities. The
spectacle of a big bank's liquidity drying up in just a few days made exchanges and brokerage houses wary of
their trading partners. They raised margin requirements, or the collateral required when investors borrow from
their brokers, to protect against risky trades. There was a wave of "speculative liquidation," as many
short-term speculators chose to close out their positions to take profit or to get cash for collateral to maintain
their positions.
The steep selloff that followed Bear's meltdown swept across the entire commodity complex. Gold dropped
$58.50 to $944.79 on March 19, its biggest decline in dollar terms since Jan. 22, 1980, and it fell as far as
$918.70 on March 24. Crude-oil futures fell $4.94 to $104.48 a barrel on March 19, its biggest dollar drop in
more than 17 years. Grains and soft commodities, which had been running up faster, took a deeper hit.
Although volatility isn't unusual in commodities markets, declines of this magnitude had been rarely seen,
said John Brynjolfsson, a portfolio manager and managing director at Pacific Investment Management Co.,
which runs the Pimco Commodity Real Return Strategy Fund.
The late-March declines led to some soul-searching among investors. "The easy money in commodities trade
is over," said William Flaig, chief investment officer of Arrow Funds, whose Arrow Alternative Solutions Fund
invests in commodity futures. "Just indiscriminately buying any commodity and getting a good outcome, I
think, is over."

--- Page 221 ---
Page 221 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Still, many analysts believe the recent declines offer a buying opportunity. They say there has been little
change in underlying fundamentals -- namely, growing demand from emerging economies and a tight supply
situation, while the selloff has forced out some short-term speculative buyers and brought prices down to
more consistent levels.
Even if the broad rally in commodities may be slowing, analysts say there are opportunities within individual
sectors. Mr. Brynjolfsson thinks natural gas, which rose 35% in the quarter on the Nymex, has more upward
potential, as it might see increasing demand from power companies because of its lower cost compared with
oil and its more environmentally friendly profile.
Mr. Flaig believes agricultural products will continue to outperform other sectors for the rest of the year. In
March, U.S. wheat inventories were at their lowest levels since 1946-47, according to a World Agricultural
Supply and Demand Estimates report.
"Our long-term structurally bullish fundamental views on the grains remain unchanged," Goldman Sachs
Group Inc. wrote in a research note, saying that recent price declines create a "potential buying opportunity."
Meanwhile, analysts also warn against some high-priced commodities, such as palladium, which, like
platinum, is used to make catalytic converters for cars. Bid up mainly by speculative buyers, the metal soared
55% in late February, before slipping to its current price: 19% higher than at the beginning of this year. The
palladium market has posted a surplus of more than one million ounces for five consecutive years, and there
are more than two years of above-ground inventories relative to consumption, according to the Barclays
Capital unit of Barclays PLC. unit of
"Even on a best-case scenario for fundamentals, it is difficult to justify the recent increase in palladium
prices," Barclays said in a research note.

Treasurys Get Boost From Timely Buying
Credit Markets
Treasurys Get Boost From Timely Buying
By Min Zeng
675 words
1 April 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Treasurys closed out the first quarter on a high note, supported by quarter-end flows and month-end portfolio
rebalancing amid worries about further write-downs by major financial institutions.
Buying in Treasurys began early in the session, following a report by Merrill Lynch forecasting that UBS AG
will make further write-downs in the first quarter and a profit warning from Deutsche Bank AG. forecasting that
UBS AG will make further write-downs in the first quarter and a profit warning from
Quarter- and month-end flows into the Treasury market also helped. Many hedge funds and mutual funds
need to rebalance their portfolios to match changes in the indexes against which they measure their
performance. In many cases, that means fund managers need to buy newly minted debt to replace debt that
has matured or moved out of the benchmark. That is referred to as duration buying. Duration measures the
sensitivity of changes in bond prices to changes in interest rates.
Monday's rally, led by debt maturing in at least five years, was also boosted by a selloff in commodities, with
crude oil and gold prices tumbling, as some investors decided to return to the comfort of low-risk government
debt or to dress up their balance sheets at the end of the quarter.
Light trading volume on the last day of the month may also have exaggerated some of the price moves,
traders said. "You are seeing some buying especially (at) the back end of the curve. Some duration-related
buying is going on at month end," said Paul Horrmann, a bond-market strategist at ICAP PLC, an interdealer
broker.
The benchmark 10-year note gained 9/32 point, or $2.8125 for every $1,000 invested, to yield 3.432%. That's
down from 3.464%, as bond yields fall when prices rise.
Treasurys have beaten riskier competitors ranging from stocks and corporate bonds to mortgage-backed
securities since the start of the year amid uncertainty about the credit-market crunch and faltering growth.
Yields on two-, five- and 10-year Treasurys fell to their weakest point since mid-2003. The bond equivalent
yields on three-month T-bills plunged to a five-decade low this month.
From the beginning of the year through Friday, returns on Treasurys were 4.19% compared with returns of
2.3% on mortgage-backed securities, according to data from Lehman Brothers. High-yield corporate debt, or
junk bonds, posted a negative return of 2.78% over the same period. Even the municipal-debt sector posted a
negative return of 0.79%, due to the turmoil in the auction-rate securities market and forced liquidations from
hedge funds.

World News: Mugabe Rival Claims Lead --- Zimbabwe President Warns Opposition; Runoff on Horizon?
World News: Mugabe Rival Claims Lead --- Zimbabwe President Warns Opposition; Runoff on
Horizon?
By Sarah Childress
526 words
31 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A8
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Zimbabwe's opposition said Sunday it was ahead in presidential elections held the day before, drawing a
quick warning from the government of President Robert Mugabe that any early claim of victory amounted to a
coup attempt.
The opposition Movement for Democratic Change said preliminary results in urban areas showed that their
leader, Morgan Tsvangirai, was ahead, based on the party's surveys of initial results. It was impossible to
substantiate those claims. Official results hadn't been announced.
Reports of rampant vote manipulation by the government ahead of the polls and widespread accusations of
manipulation in previous elections have most outside observers expecting official results to once again favor
Mr. Mugabe. An early lead by the opposition in the last elections in 2002 quickly evaporated.
But the verbal clash raised tensions on both sides in the aftermath of the vote. In the past, Mr. Mugabe has
used violence to intimidate opposition members, according to widely reported accounts of previous elections.
According to the constitution, a candidate needs at least 51% of the vote to win. If no candidate earns a
majority, a runoff must be held between the top two vote-getters.
A third candidate in this election is Simba Makoni, Mr. Mugabe's former finance minister.
The Saturday vote itself was relatively peaceful, although observers reported that the names of several
thousand dead or nonexistent "ghost voters" had appeared on the rolls. The opposition said some of its
supporters had been turned away from the polls.
For critics of Mr. Mugabe, the election was a chance to end his 28-year rule, which he began in triumph as a
hero who liberated Zimbabwe from racist white rule. In recent years, though, Mr. Mugabe has presided over a
country that has withered under corruption and mismanagement.
Some analysts had expected Messrs. Makoni and Tsvangirai to split the opposition vote and hand another
win to the president. One possible outcome, if either opposition candidate makes significant gains, is that Mr.
Mugabe could be forced into a runoff. In that case, his opponents are expected to form a unity ticket.
In the run-up to this year's vote, a Human Rights Watch report said that ruling party supporters were bribing
rural voters with food and beating opposition supporters.
Watchdog groups predicted that Mr. Mugabe would attempt to fix the vote so that he doesn't have to face a
second round. Mr. Mugabe has said he has no need to cheat because he has the people's support.
The real challenge will come after the election, when either Mr. Mugabe or one of his opponents will have to
resuscitate Zimbabwe's flat-lining economy. Inflation is at about 100,000%, the highest in the world, and
infrastructure has crumbled. Access to electricity and running water is scarce, and store shelves are empty.
Another hurdle will be interpreting a new bill, signed into law by Mr. Mugabe this month, that allows locals to
take majority shares in foreign companies. Companies, including the major platinum and gold mining
operations, are watching closely.

Some Pension Funds Do More Than Dabble --- Commodity Markets Become New Arena For Active Investing
Commodities Report
Some Pension Funds Do More Than Dabble --- Commodity Markets Become New Arena For Active
Investing
By Gregory Meyer
701 words
31 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
When it comes to commodities markets, pension funds are no longer simply onlookers.
While pension funds wading deep into commodities remain a rarity, those that do invest are increasingly
actively managing their positions, rather than adopting a passive stance that assumes prices will inexorably
rise.
The largest fund in the U.S., the California Public Employees' Retirement System, or Calpers, made waves
last month when its board authorized putting as much as 3% of its $240 billion portfolio in commodities.
Moreover, the newly adopted investment policy for commodities allows that "the risk exposure of the
investment instruments may be long or short, or a combination of both."
"Why would we overlook any potential strategy that would give us good returns?" said Clark McKinley, a
Calpers spokesman.
Calpers's move doesn't signal a gold rush by pension funds. The nation's second- and third-largest state
pension funds -- the $166.5 billion California State Teachers' Retirement System and the $154.5 billion New
York State Common Retirement Fund -- have so far kept away from direct investments in futures and options
contracts tied to commodities.
The handful of pension funds that have moved into commodities have done so for two main reasons. One is
that commodities generally don't track prices of stocks and bonds, which helps diversify a portfolio. They also
tend to keep in front of inflation, a priority for funds whose retiree payouts are pegged to the consumer-price
index.
Pension funds initially sought to follow the main commodities indexes, the Dow Jones-AIG Commodity Index
and the Standard & Poor's GSCI Index, as investment products tracking them proliferated. Calpers began
investing directly in commodities last May, and has nearly $500 million tracking the S&P GSCI Total Return
Index.
"Later this year, we plan to begin using some commodities-investment strategies beyond what we've done
with the index on a 'pilot' basis," Mr. McKinley, the Calpers spokesman, said.
A growing number of funds now use active managers to run at least parts of their commodity portfolios, not
least because hitching assets to an index can create big losses when volatile markets go south. "Rolling"
expiring commodity futures contracts from one month to the next also can cut into returns when later-month
contracts cost more than current ones.
Among North American pension funds and other institutional investors recently surveyed by pension-fund
adviser Russell Investments, 11% said they were passively invested in commodities and 6% said they were
actively invested in the asset class. But 20% of respondents said they are considering active investments in
commodities, which is more than those considering passive commodity investments.
Investment officers at major pension plans say they see long-term growth in demand for commodities, fed in
part by China and other emerging economies, that will push prices higher for years to come.
"The emerging markets are demanding more and more commodities," said Alan Van Noord, chief investment
officer at the Pennsylvania Public School Employees' Retirement System, which holds about $2.3 billion of its
$63 billion in assets in commodities. As well, he said, the Federal Reserve "has sort of relaxed [its]
inflationary stance and instead is looking to stimulate the economy. You could have sort of a super-cycle
developing with inflationary pressures that last more than a short-term cyclical uptick."

His board approved the commodity-investment program in late 2006, and now seeks to hold 5% of the plan's
assets in commodities. About 80% of it is actively managed.
"Our managers are allowed to take positions against or for, overweight or underweight" the Dow Jones-AIG,
he said.
The Ontario Teachers' Pension Plan, which has about 2% of its 106 billion Canadian dollars (US$104 billion)
in investments allocated to commodities, attempts to outrun the GSCI.
"We do opportunistic trading in the commodities space. Our intent is to earn something above the index," said
Neil Petroff, group vice president of investments at the Ontario fund, where an in-house staff of seven
manages the commodities portfolio. "It may involve stocks, bonds, futures, swaps and long and short
positions."
